</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Software_development_process" title="Template:Software development process"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Software_development_process" title="Template talk:Software development process"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Software_development_process" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Software development process"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Agile software development</b> is an umbrella term for approaches to <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">developing software</a> that reflect the values and principles agreed upon by <i>The Agile Alliance</i>, a group of 17 software practitioners, in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As documented in their <i>Manifesto for Agile Software Development</i> the practitioners value:<sup id="cite_ref-AgileManifesto_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AgileManifesto-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p>
<ul><li><b>Individuals and interactions</b> over processes and tools</li>
<li><b>Working software</b> over comprehensive documentation</li>
<li><b>Customer collaboration</b> over contract negotiation</li>
<li><b>Responding to change</b> over following a plan</li></ul>
<p>The practitioners cite inspiration from new practices at the time including <a href="/wiki/Extreme_programming" title="Extreme programming">extreme programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scrum_(software_development)" title="Scrum (software development)">scrum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_systems_development_method" title="Dynamic systems development method">dynamic systems development method</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_software_development" title="Adaptive software development">adaptive software development</a> and being sympathetic to the need for an alternative to documentation driven, heavyweight software development processes.<sup id="cite_ref-LarmanGuide_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LarmanGuide-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Many software development practices emerged from the agile mindset. These agile-based practices, sometimes called <i>Agile</i> (with a capital A)<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> include requirements, discovery and solutions improvement through the collaborative effort of <a href="/wiki/Self-organizing_communities" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-organizing communities">self-organizing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cross-functional_team" title="Cross-functional team">cross-functional</a> teams with their <a href="/wiki/Customer" title="Customer">customer(s)</a>/<a href="/wiki/End_user" title="End user">end user(s)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollier2011_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollier2011-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WhatIsAgile_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WhatIsAgile-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While there is much <a href="/wiki/Anecdotal_evidence" title="Anecdotal evidence">anecdotal evidence</a> that the agile mindset and agile-based practices improve the software development process, the empirical evidence is limited and less than conclusive.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" /></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iterative_and_incremental_development" title="Iterative and incremental development">Iterative and incremental software development methods</a> can be traced back as early as 1957,<sup id="cite_ref-craig2003_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-craig2003-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with evolutionary project management<sup id="cite_ref-EvolutionaryProjectManagement_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EvolutionaryProjectManagement-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_software_development" title="Adaptive software development">adaptive software development</a><sup id="cite_ref-edmonds1974_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edmonds1974-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> emerging in the early 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1990s, a number of <i>lightweight</i> software development methods evolved in reaction to the prevailing <i>heavyweight</i> methods (often referred to collectively as <a href="/wiki/Waterfall_model" title="Waterfall model"><i>waterfall</i></a>) that critics described as overly regulated, planned, and <a href="/wiki/Micromanagement" title="Micromanagement">micromanaged</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These lightweight methods included: <a href="/wiki/Rapid_application_development" title="Rapid application development">rapid application development</a> (RAD), from 1991;<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Unified_Process" class="mw-redirect" title="Unified Process">unified process</a> (UP) and <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_systems_development_method" title="Dynamic systems development method">dynamic systems development method</a> (DSDM), both from 1994; <a href="/wiki/Scrum_(software_development)" title="Scrum (software development)">Scrum</a>, from 1995; Crystal Clear and <a href="/wiki/Extreme_programming" title="Extreme programming">extreme programming</a> (XP), both from 1996; and <a href="/wiki/Feature-driven_development" title="Feature-driven development">feature-driven development</a> (FDD), from 1997. Although these all originated before the publication of the <i>Agile Manifesto</i>, they are now collectively referred to as agile software development methods.<sup id="cite_ref-LarmanGuide_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LarmanGuide-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Already since 1991 similar changes had been underway in <a href="/wiki/Agile_manufacturing" title="Agile manufacturing">manufacturing</a><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Agile_management" title="Agile management">management thinking</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> derived from <a href="/wiki/Lean_manufacturing" title="Lean manufacturing">Lean management</a>.
</p><p>In 2001, seventeen software developers met at a resort in <a href="/wiki/Snowbird,_Utah" title="Snowbird, Utah">Snowbird</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> to discuss lightweight development methods. They were: <a href="/wiki/Kent_Beck" title="Kent Beck">Kent Beck</a> (Extreme Programming), <a href="/wiki/Ward_Cunningham" title="Ward Cunningham">Ward Cunningham</a> (Extreme Programming), <a href="/wiki/Dave_Thomas_(programmer)" title="Dave Thomas (programmer)">Dave Thomas</a> (<a href="/wiki/The_Pragmatic_Programmer" title="The Pragmatic Programmer">Pragmatic Programming</a>, Ruby), <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Sutherland" title="Jeff Sutherland">Jeff Sutherland</a> (Scrum), <a href="/wiki/Ken_Schwaber" title="Ken Schwaber">Ken Schwaber</a> (Scrum), <a href="/wiki/Jim_Highsmith" title="Jim Highsmith">Jim Highsmith</a> (Adaptive Software Development), <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Cockburn" title="Alistair Cockburn">Alistair Cockburn</a> (Crystal), <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Martin" title="Robert C. Martin">Robert C. Martin</a> (<a href="/wiki/SOLID" title="SOLID">SOLID</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mike_Beedle" title="Mike Beedle">Mike Beedle</a> (Scrum), Arie van Bennekum, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Fowler_(software_engineer)" title="Martin Fowler (software engineer)">Martin Fowler</a> (<a href="/wiki/OOAD" class="mw-redirect" title="OOAD">OOAD</a> and <a href="/wiki/UML" class="mw-redirect" title="UML">UML</a>), James Grenning, <a href="/wiki/Andy_Hunt_(author)" title="Andy Hunt (author)">Andrew Hunt</a> (Pragmatic Programming, Ruby), <a href="/wiki/Ron_Jeffries" title="Ron Jeffries">Ron Jeffries</a> (Extreme Programming), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jon_Kern&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jon Kern (page does not exist)">Jon Kern</a>, Brian Marick (Ruby, <a href="/wiki/Test-driven_development" title="Test-driven development">Test-driven development</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_J._Mellor" title="Stephen J. Mellor">Steve Mellor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shlaer%E2%80%93Mellor_method" title="Shlaer–Mellor method">OOA</a>). The group, The Agile Alliance, published the <i>Manifesto for Agile Software Development</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-AgileManifesto_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AgileManifesto-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, a group headed by Cockburn and Highsmith wrote an addendum of <a href="/wiki/Project_management" title="Project management">project management</a> principles, the PM Declaration of Interdependence,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to guide software project management according to agile software development methods.
</p><p>In 2009, a group working with Martin wrote an extension of <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">software development</a> principles, the <a href="/wiki/Software_craftsmanship" title="Software craftsmanship">Software Craftsmanship Manifesto</a>, to guide agile software development according to <a href="/wiki/Professional" title="Professional">professional</a> conduct and mastery.
</p><p>In 2011, the Agile Alliance created the <i>Guide to Agile Practices</i> (renamed the <i>Agile Glossary</i> in 2016),<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> an evolving <a href="/wiki/Open_source" title="Open source">open-source</a> compendium of the working definitions of agile practices, terms, and elements, along with interpretations and experience guidelines from the worldwide community of agile practitioners.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Values_and_principles">Values and principles <span class="anchor" id="The_Agile_Manifesto"></span></h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Values and principles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Values">Values</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Values"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The agile manifesto reads:<sup id="cite_ref-AgileManifesto_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AgileManifesto-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><i>We are uncovering better ways of developing software by doing it and helping others do it. Through this work we have come to value:</i>
</p>
<ul><li><em> <strong> Individuals and interactions</strong> over processes and tools </em></li>
<li><em> <strong> Working software</strong> over comprehensive documentation </em></li>
<li><em> <strong> Customer collaboration</strong> over contract negotiation </em></li>
<li><em> <strong> Responding to change</strong> over following a plan </em></li></ul>
<p><i>That is, while there is value in the items on the right, we value the items on the left more.</i>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Scott_Ambler" title="Scott Ambler">Scott Ambler</a> explained:<sup id="cite_ref-abmmw_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abmmw-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Tools and processes are important, but it is more important to have competent people working together effectively.</li>
<li>Good documentation is useful in helping people to understand how the software is built and how to use it, but the main point of development is to create software, not documentation.</li>
<li>A contract is important but is not a substitute for working closely with customers to discover what they need.</li>
<li>A project plan is important, but it must not be too rigid to accommodate changes in technology or the environment, stakeholders' priorities, and people's understanding of the problem and its solution.</li></ul>
<p>Introducing the manifesto on behalf of the Agile Alliance, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Highsmith" title="Jim Highsmith">Jim Highsmith</a> said,
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Agile movement is not anti-methodology, in fact many of us want to restore credibility to the word methodology. We want to restore a balance. We embrace modeling, but not in order to file some diagram in a dusty corporate repository. We embrace documentation, but not hundreds of pages of never-maintained and rarely-used tomes. We plan, but recognize the limits of planning in a turbulent environment. Those who would brand proponents of XP or SCRUM or any of the other Agile Methodologies as "hackers" are ignorant of both the methodologies and the original definition of the term hacker.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8202;<cite>Jim Highsmith, History: The Agile Manifesto<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Principles"><span class="anchor" id="Agile_principles"></span> Principles</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Principles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The values are based on these principles:<sup id="cite_ref-ManifestoPrinciples_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ManifestoPrinciples-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Customer satisfaction by early and continuous delivery of valuable software.</li>
<li>Welcome changing requirements, even in late development.</li>
<li>Deliver working software frequently (weeks rather than months).</li>
<li>Close, daily cooperation between business people and developers.</li>
<li>Projects are built around motivated individuals, who should be trusted.</li>
<li>Face-to-face conversation is the best form of communication (co-location).</li>
<li>Working software is the primary measure of progress.</li>
<li>Sustainable development, able to maintain a constant pace.</li>
<li>Continuous attention to technical excellence and good design.</li>
<li>Simplicity—the art of maximizing the amount of work not done—is essential.</li>
<li>Best <a href="/wiki/Agile_Architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Agile Architecture">architectures</a>, requirements, and designs emerge from self-organizing teams.</li>
<li>Regularly, the team reflects on how to become more effective, and adjusts accordingly.</li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Overview">Overview</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Iterative,_incremental,_and_evolutionary"><span id="Iterative.2C_incremental.2C_and_evolutionary"></span>Iterative, incremental, and evolutionary</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Iterative, incremental, and evolutionary"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Most agile development methods break product development work into small increments that minimize the amount of up-front planning and design. Iterations, or sprints, are short time frames (<a href="/wiki/Timeboxing" title="Timeboxing">timeboxes</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEProject_Management_Institute20212.3.3_Development_Approaches_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEProject_Management_Institute20212.3.3_Development_Approaches-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that typically last from one to four weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERubin2013_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERubin2013-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 20">&#58;&#8202;20&#8202;</span></sup> Each iteration involves a <a href="/wiki/Cross-functional_team" title="Cross-functional team">cross-functional team</a> working in all functions: <a href="/wiki/Project_planning" title="Project planning">planning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Requirements_analysis" title="Requirements analysis">analysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_design" title="Software design">design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">coding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unit_testing" title="Unit testing">unit testing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acceptance_testing" title="Acceptance testing">acceptance testing</a>. At the end of the iteration a working product is demonstrated to stakeholders. This minimizes overall risk and allows the product to adapt to changes quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEProject_Management_Institute2021§3.12_Enable_Change_to_Achieve_the_Envisioned_Future_State_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEProject_Management_Institute2021§3.12_Enable_Change_to_Achieve_the_Envisioned_Future_State-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An iteration might not add enough functionality to warrant a market release, but the goal is to have an available release (with minimal <a href="/wiki/Software_bug" title="Software bug">bugs</a>) at the end of each iteration.<sup id="cite_ref-embracing_change_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embracing_change-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Through incremental development, products have room to "<a href="/wiki/Fail-fast_system" title="Fail-fast system">fail often and early</a>" throughout each iterative phase instead of drastically on a final release date.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Multiple iterations might be required to release a product or new features. Working software is the primary measure of progress.<sup id="cite_ref-ManifestoPrinciples_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ManifestoPrinciples-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A key advantage of agile approaches is speed to market and risk mitigation. Smaller increments are typically released to market, reducing the time and cost risks of engineering a product that doesn't meet user requirements.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Efficient_and_face-to-face_communication">Efficient and face-to-face communication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Efficient and face-to-face communication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The 6th principle of the agile manifesto for software development states "The most efficient and effective method of conveying information to and within a development team is face-to-face conversation". The manifesto, written in 2001 when video conferencing was not widely used, states this in relation to the communication of information, not necessarily that a team should be co-located.
</p><p>The principle of <a href="/wiki/Colocation_(business)" title="Colocation (business)">co-location</a> is that co-workers on the same team should be situated together to better establish the identity as a team and to improve communication.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This enables <a href="/wiki/Face-to-face_interaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Face-to-face interaction">face-to-face interaction</a>, ideally in front of a whiteboard, that reduces the cycle time typically taken when questions and answers are mediated through phone, <a href="/wiki/Persistent_Chat" title="Persistent Chat">persistent chat</a>, wiki, or email.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the widespread adoption of remote working during the COVID-19 pandemic and changes to tooling, more studies have been conducted<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> around co-location and distributed working which show that co-location is increasingly less relevant.
</p><p>No matter which development method is followed, every team should include a <a href="/wiki/Customer_representative" title="Customer representative">customer representative</a> (known as <i>product owner</i> in <a href="/wiki/Scrum_(software_development)" title="Scrum (software development)">Scrum</a>). This representative is agreed by stakeholders to act on their behalf and makes a personal commitment to being available for developers to answer questions throughout the iteration. At the end of each iteration, the <a href="/wiki/Project_stakeholder" title="Project stakeholder">project stakeholders</a> together with the customer representative review progress and re-evaluate priorities with a view to optimizing the <a href="/wiki/Rate_of_return" title="Rate of return">return on investment</a> (ROI) and ensuring alignment with customer needs and company goals. The importance of stakeholder satisfaction, detailed by frequent interaction and review at the end of each phase, is why the approach is often denoted as a <a href="/wiki/Customer_centricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Customer centricity">customer-centered methodology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Information_radiator">Information radiator</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Information radiator"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In agile software development, an <b>information radiator</b> is a (normally large) physical display, board with <a href="/wiki/Sticky_note" class="mw-redirect" title="Sticky note">sticky notes</a> or similar, located prominently near the development team, where passers-by can see it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEProject_Management_Institute2021§2.7.3.2_Information_Radiators_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEProject_Management_Institute2021§2.7.3.2_Information_Radiators-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It presents an up-to-date summary of the product development status.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambler_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambler-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Build_light_indicator" title="Build light indicator">build light indicator</a> may also be used to inform a team about the current status of their product development.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Very_short_feedback_loop_and_adaptation_cycle">Very short feedback loop and adaptation cycle</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Very short feedback loop and adaptation cycle"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A common characteristic in agile software development is the <a href="/wiki/Stand-up_meeting" title="Stand-up meeting">daily stand-up</a> (known as <i>daily scrum</i> in the Scrum framework). In a brief session (e.g., 15 minutes), team members review collectively how they are progressing toward their goal and agree whether they need to adapt their approach. To keep to the agreed time limit, teams often use simple coded questions (such as what they completed the previous day, what they aim to complete that day, and whether there are any impediments or risks to progress), and delay detailed discussions and problem resolution until after the stand-up.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Quality_focus">Quality focus</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Quality focus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pair_programming_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Pair_programming_1.jpg/220px-Pair_programming_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Pair_programming_1.jpg/330px-Pair_programming_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Pair_programming_1.jpg/440px-Pair_programming_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Pair_programming" title="Pair programming">Pair programming</a>, an agile development technique used in <a href="/wiki/Extreme_Programming" class="mw-redirect" title="Extreme Programming">XP</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Specific tools and techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Continuous_integration" title="Continuous integration">continuous integration</a>, automated <a href="/wiki/Unit_testing" title="Unit testing">unit testing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pair_programming" title="Pair programming">pair programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Test-driven_development" title="Test-driven development">test-driven development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_design_pattern" title="Software design pattern">design patterns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Behavior-driven_development" title="Behavior-driven development">behavior-driven development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domain-driven_design" title="Domain-driven design">domain-driven design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Code_refactoring" title="Code refactoring">code refactoring</a> and other techniques are often used to improve quality and enhance product development agility.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is predicated on designing and building quality in from the beginning and being able to demonstrate software for customers at any point, or at least at the end of every iteration.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Philosophy">Philosophy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Philosophy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Compared to traditional software engineering, agile software development mainly targets complex systems and product development with dynamic, <a href="/wiki/Indeterministic" class="mw-redirect" title="Indeterministic">indeterministic</a> and non-linear <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)"> properties</a>. Accurate estimates, stable plans, and predictions are often hard to get in early stages, and confidence in them is likely to be low. Agile practitioners use their <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> to reduce the "<a href="/wiki/Leap_of_faith" title="Leap of faith">leap of faith</a>" that is needed before any <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> of <a href="/wiki/Value_(ethics_and_social_sciences)" class="mw-redirect" title="Value (ethics and social sciences)">value</a> can be obtained.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell2016_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2016-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Requirements and design are held to be <a href="/wiki/Emergentism" title="Emergentism">emergent</a>. Big up-front specifications would probably <a href="/wiki/Cause" class="mw-redirect" title="Cause">cause</a> a lot of waste in such cases, i.e., are not economically sound. These basic <a href="/wiki/Arguments" class="mw-redirect" title="Arguments">arguments</a> and previous industry <a href="/wiki/Experiences" class="mw-redirect" title="Experiences">experiences</a>, learned from years of successes and failures, have helped shape agile development's favor of adaptive, iterative and evolutionary development.<sup id="cite_ref-Larman2004_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Larman2004-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Adaptive_vs._predictive">Adaptive vs. predictive</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Adaptive vs. predictive"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Development methods exist on a continuum from <i>adaptive</i> to <i>predictive</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-boehm2004App_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boehm2004App-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Agile software development methods lie on the <i>adaptive</i> side of this continuum. One key of adaptive development methods is a <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Wave_planning" class="mw-redirect" title="Rolling Wave planning"><i>rolling wave</i></a> approach to schedule planning, which identifies milestones but leaves flexibility in the path to reach them, and also allows for the milestones themselves to change.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><i>Adaptive</i> methods focus on adapting quickly to changing realities. When the needs of a project change, an adaptive team changes as well. An adaptive team has difficulty describing exactly what will happen in the future. The further away a date is, the more vague an adaptive method is about what will happen on that date. An adaptive team cannot report exactly what tasks they will do next week, but only which features they plan for next month. When asked about a release six months from now, an adaptive team might be able to report only the mission statement for the release, or a statement of expected value vs. cost.
</p><p><i>Predictive</i> methods, in contrast, focus on analyzing and planning the future in detail and cater for known risks. In the extremes, a predictive team can report exactly what features and tasks are planned for the entire length of the development process. Predictive methods rely on effective early phase analysis, and if this goes very wrong, the project may have difficulty changing direction. Predictive teams often institute a <a href="/wiki/Change_control_board" title="Change control board">change control board</a> to ensure they consider only the most valuable changes.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Risk_analysis_(engineering)" class="mw-redirect" title="Risk analysis (engineering)">Risk analysis</a> can be used to choose between adaptive (<i>agile</i> or <i>value-driven</i>) and predictive (<i>plan-driven</i>) methods.<sup id="cite_ref-PMBridgeToAgility_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PMBridgeToAgility-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barry_Boehm" title="Barry Boehm">Barry Boehm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Turner_(computer_scientist)" title="Richard Turner (computer scientist)">Richard Turner</a> suggest that each side of the continuum has its own <i>home ground</i>, as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-boehm2004_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boehm2004-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Home grounds of different development methods
</caption>

</th>
<th style="width: 33%">Plan-driven methods (waterfall)
</th>

</td>
<td>Culture that demands order
</td>
<td>Extreme quality
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Agile_vs._waterfall">Agile vs. waterfall</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Agile vs. waterfall"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One of the differences between agile software development methods and waterfall is the approach to quality and testing. In the <a href="/wiki/Waterfall_model" title="Waterfall model">waterfall model</a>, work moves through <a href="/wiki/Software_development_life_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Software development life cycle">software development life cycle</a> (SDLC) phases—with one phase being completed before another can start—hence the <i><b>testing phase</b></i> is separate and follows a <i><b>build phase</b></i>. In agile software development, however, testing is completed in the same iteration as programming.
</p><p>Because testing is done in every iteration—which develops a small piece of the software—users can frequently use those new pieces of software and validate the value. After the users know the real value of the updated piece of software, they can make better decisions about the software's future. Having a value retrospective and software re-planning session in each iteration—<a href="/wiki/Scrum_(software_development)" title="Scrum (software development)">Scrum</a> typically has iterations of just two weeks—helps the team continuously adapt its plans so as to maximize the value it delivers. This follows a pattern similar to the <a href="/wiki/PDCA" title="PDCA">plan-do-check-act</a> (PDCA) cycle, as the work is <i>planned</i>, <i>done</i>, <i>checked</i> (in the review and retrospective), and any changes agreed are <i>acted</i> upon.
</p><p>This iterative approach supports a <i>product</i> rather than a <i>project</i> mindset. This provides greater flexibility throughout the development process; whereas on projects the requirements are defined and locked down from the very beginning, making it difficult to change them later. Iterative product development allows the software to evolve in response to changes in business environment or market requirements.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Code_vs._documentation">Code vs. documentation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Code vs. documentation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In a letter to <i><a href="/wiki/Computer_(magazine)" title="Computer (magazine)">IEEE Computer</a></i>, Steven Rakitin expressed cynicism about agile software development, calling it "<i>yet another attempt to undermine the discipline of software engineering</i>" and translating "<i>working software over comprehensive documentation</i>" as "<i>we want to spend all our time coding. Remember, real programmers don't write documentation</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-rakitin2001_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rakitin2001-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This is disputed by proponents of agile software development, who state that developers should write documentation if that is the best way to achieve the relevant goals, but that there are often better ways to achieve those goals than writing static documentation.<sup id="cite_ref-agiledoc_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agiledoc-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Scott_Ambler" title="Scott Ambler">Scott Ambler</a> states that documentation should be "just barely good enough" (JBGE),<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that too much or comprehensive documentation would usually cause waste, and developers rarely trust detailed documentation because it's usually out of sync with code,<sup id="cite_ref-agiledoc_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agiledoc-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while too little documentation may also cause problems for maintenance, communication, learning and knowledge sharing. <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Cockburn" title="Alistair Cockburn">Alistair Cockburn</a> wrote of the <i>Crystal Clear</i> method: 
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Crystal considers development a series of co-operative games, and intends that the documentation is enough to help the next win at the next game. The work products for Crystal include use cases, risk list, iteration plan, core domain models, and design notes to inform on choices...however there are no templates for these documents and descriptions are necessarily vague, but the objective is clear, <b>just enough documentation</b> for the next game. I always tend to characterize this to my team as: what would you want to know if you joined the team tomorrow.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8202;<cite>Alistair Cockburn<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Methods">Methods</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Methods"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SoftwareDevelopmentLifeCycle.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/SoftwareDevelopmentLifeCycle.jpg/220px-SoftwareDevelopmentLifeCycle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/SoftwareDevelopmentLifeCycle.jpg/330px-SoftwareDevelopmentLifeCycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/SoftwareDevelopmentLifeCycle.jpg/440px-SoftwareDevelopmentLifeCycle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="726" data-file-height="494" /></a><figcaption>Software development life cycle support<sup id="cite_ref-Abrahamsson2002_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrahamsson2002-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Unified_Process_Model_for_Iterative_Development.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Unified_Process_Model_for_Iterative_Development.svg/220px-Unified_Process_Model_for_Iterative_Development.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Unified_Process_Model_for_Iterative_Development.svg/330px-Unified_Process_Model_for_Iterative_Development.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Unified_Process_Model_for_Iterative_Development.svg/440px-Unified_Process_Model_for_Iterative_Development.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1052" data-file-height="744" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Agile_unified_process" title="Agile unified process">Agile unified process</a> (AUP) is based on <a href="/wiki/Unified_process" title="Unified process">unified process</a> (an iterative and incremental software development process framework).</figcaption></figure> 
<p>Agile software development methods support a broad range of the <a href="/wiki/Software_development_life_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Software development life cycle">software development life cycle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrahamsson2002_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrahamsson2002-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some methods focus on the practices (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Extreme_programming" title="Extreme programming">XP</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Pragmatic_Programmer" title="The Pragmatic Programmer">pragmatic programming</a>, agile modeling), while some focus on managing the flow of work (e.g., Scrum, Kanban). Some support activities for requirements specification and development (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Feature-driven_development" title="Feature-driven development">FDD</a>), while some seek to cover the full development life cycle (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_systems_development_method" title="Dynamic systems development method">DSDM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rational_Unified_Process" class="mw-redirect" title="Rational Unified Process">RUP</a>).
</p><p>Notable agile software development frameworks include:
