<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Acceptance_test-driven_development">Acceptance test-driven development</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Acceptance test-driven development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Acceptance_test-driven_development" title="Acceptance test-driven development">Acceptance test-driven development</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Acceptance_test-driven_development&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Acceptance_test-driven_development" title="Acceptance test-driven development">Acceptance test–driven development</a> (ATDD) is a <a href="/wiki/Software_development" title="Software development">development</a> methodology based on communication between the business customers, the developers, and the testers.<sup id="cite_ref-Acceptance_test-driven_development_Pugh11_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acceptance_test-driven_development_Pugh11-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> ATDD encompasses many of the same practices as <a href="/wiki/Specification_by_example" title="Specification by example">specification by example</a> (SBE),<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Behavior-driven_development" title="Behavior-driven development">behavior-driven development</a> (BDD),<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> example-driven development (EDD),<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and support-driven development also called story test–driven development (SDD).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> All these processes aid developers and testers in understanding the customer's needs prior to implementation and allow customers to be able to converse in their own domain language.</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Agile_modeling">Agile modeling</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Agile modeling"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Agile_modeling" title="Agile modeling">Agile modeling</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Agile_modeling&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Agile_modeling" title="Agile modeling">Agile modeling</a> (AM) is a methodology for <a href="/wiki/Software_development_process_models" class="mw-redirect" title="Software development process models">modeling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Documentation" title="Documentation">documenting</a> software systems based on best practices. It is a collection of values and principles that can be applied on an (agile) software development project. This methodology is more flexible than traditional modeling methods, making it a better fit in a fast-changing environment.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is part of the agile software development tool kit.</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Agile_testing">Agile testing</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Agile testing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Agile_testing" title="Agile testing">Agile testing</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Agile_testing&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Agile_testing" title="Agile testing">Agile testing</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Software_testing" title="Software testing">software testing</a> practice that follows the principles of agile software development. Agile testing involves all members of a cross-functional agile team, with special expertise contributed by testers, to ensure delivering the business value desired by the customer at frequent intervals, working at a sustainable pace. <a href="/wiki/Specification_by_example" title="Specification by example">Specification by example</a> is used to capture examples of desired and undesired behavior and guide coding.</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Backlogs">Backlogs</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Backlogs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Product_backlog" title="Product backlog">Product backlog</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Product_backlog&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
Within <a href="/wiki/Agile_project_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Agile project management">agile project management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Product_backlog" title="Product backlog">product backlog</a> refers to a prioritized list of functionality which a product should contain. It is sometimes referred to as a <a href="/wiki/To-do_list" class="mw-redirect" title="To-do list">to-do list</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and is considered an 'artifact' (a form of documentation) within the <a href="/wiki/Scrum_(software_development)" title="Scrum (software development)">scrum</a> software development framework.<sup id="cite_ref-Product_backlog_:0_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Product_backlog_:0-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The product backlog is referred to with different names in different project management frameworks, such as <i>product backlog</i> in scrum,<sup id="cite_ref-Product_backlog_:0_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Product_backlog_:0-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Product_backlog_:1_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Product_backlog_:1-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> work item list in <a href="/wiki/Disciplined_agile_delivery" title="Disciplined agile delivery">disciplined agile</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Product_backlog_:1_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Product_backlog_:1-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and option pool in <a href="/wiki/Lean_manufacturing" title="Lean manufacturing">lean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Product_backlog_:1_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Product_backlog_:1-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the scrum framework, creation and continuous maintenance of the product backlog is part of the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/Scrum_(software_development)" title="Scrum (software development)">product owner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Behavior-driven_development">Behavior-driven development</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Behavior-driven development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Behavior-driven_development" title="Behavior-driven development">Behavior-driven development</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Behavior-driven_development&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Behavior-driven_development" title="Behavior-driven development">Behavior-driven development</a> (BDD) involves naming <a href="/wiki/Software_testing" title="Software testing">software tests</a> using <a href="/wiki/Domain_model" title="Domain model">domain language</a> to describe the behavior of the <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">code</a>.</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Continuous_integration">Continuous integration</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Continuous integration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Continuous_integration" title="Continuous integration">Continuous integration</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Continuous_integration&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Continuous_integration" title="Continuous integration">Continuous integration</a> (CI) is the practice of integrating <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">source code</a> changes frequently and ensuring that the integrated codebase is in a workable state.</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Cross-functional_team">Cross-functional team</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cross-functional team"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Cross-functional_team" title="Cross-functional team">Cross-functional team</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cross-functional_team&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
A <a href="/wiki/Cross-functional_team" title="Cross-functional team">cross-functional team</a> (XFN), also known as a multidisciplinary team or interdisciplinary team,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a group of people with different functional expertise working toward a common goal.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It may include people from <a href="/wiki/Financial_management" title="Financial management">finance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marketing_management" title="Marketing management">marketing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operations_management" title="Operations management">operations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_resource_management" title="Human resource management">human resources</a> departments. Typically, it includes employees from all levels of an organization. Members may also come from outside an organization (in particular, from suppliers, key customers, or consultants).</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Daily_stand-up">Daily stand-up</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Daily stand-up"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Stand-up_meeting" title="Stand-up meeting">Stand-up meeting</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Stand-up_meeting&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
A <a href="/wiki/Stand-up_meeting" title="Stand-up meeting">stand-up meeting</a> (stum) is a <a href="/wiki/Meeting" title="Meeting">meeting</a> in which attendees typically participate while <a href="/wiki/Standing" title="Standing">standing</a>. The discomfort of standing for long periods is intended to keep the meetings short.</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Method_tailoring">Method tailoring</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Method tailoring"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the literature, different terms refer to the notion of method adaptation, including 'method tailoring', 'method fragment adaptation' and 'situational method engineering'. Method tailoring is defined as:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A process or capability in which human agents determine a system development approach for a specific project situation through responsive changes in, and dynamic interplays between contexts, intentions, and method fragments.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8202;<cite>Mehmet Nafiz Aydin et al., An Agile Information Systems Development Method in use<sup id="cite_ref-Aydin2004_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aydin2004-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Situation-appropriateness should be considered as a distinguishing characteristic between agile methods and more plan-driven software development methods, with agile methods allowing product development teams to adapt working practices according to the needs of individual products.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aydin2004_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aydin2004-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Potentially, most agile methods could be suitable for method tailoring,<sup id="cite_ref-Abrahamsson2002_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrahamsson2002-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_Systems_Development_Method" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamic Systems Development Method">DSDM</a> tailored in a <a href="/wiki/Capability_Maturity_Model" title="Capability Maturity Model">CMM</a> context.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrahamsson2003_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrahamsson2003-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and XP tailored with the <i>Rule Description Practices</i> (RDP) technique.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Not all agile proponents agree, however, with Schwaber noting "that is how we got into trouble in the first place, thinking that the problem was not having a perfect methodology. Efforts [should] center on the changes [needed] in the enterprise".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bas Vodde reinforced this viewpoint, suggesting that unlike traditional, large methodologies that require you to pick and choose elements, Scrum provides the basics on top of which you add additional elements to localize and contextualize its use.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Practitioners seldom use <a href="/wiki/Software_development_process" title="Software development process">system development methods</a>, or agile methods specifically, by the book, often choosing to omit or tailor some of the practices of a method in order to create an in-house method.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In practice, methods can be tailored using various tools. Generic process modeling languages such as <a href="/wiki/Unified_Modeling_Language" title="Unified Modeling Language">Unified Modeling Language</a> can be used to tailor software development methods. However, dedicated tools for method engineering such as the Essence Theory of Software Engineering of <a href="/wiki/SEMAT" title="SEMAT">SEMAT</a> also exist.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Large-scale,_offshore_and_distributed"><span id="Large-scale.2C_offshore_and_distributed"></span>Large-scale, offshore and distributed</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Large-scale, offshore and distributed"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Agile software development has been widely seen as highly suited to certain types of environments, including small teams of experts working on <a href="/wiki/Greenfield_project" title="Greenfield project">greenfield projects</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-boehm2004_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boehm2004-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-beck1999_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck1999-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the challenges and limitations encountered in the adoption of agile software development methods in a large organization with <a href="/wiki/Legacy_system" title="Legacy system">legacy infrastructure</a> are well-documented and understood.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In response, a range of strategies and patterns has evolved for overcoming challenges with large-scale development efforts (&gt;20 developers)<sup id="cite_ref-ambler2006_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ambler2006-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sstc2007_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sstc2007-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or distributed (non-colocated) development teams,<sup id="cite_ref-BridgingTheDistance_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BridgingTheDistance-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AgileOffshore_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AgileOffshore-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> amongst other challenges; and there are now several recognized frameworks that seek to mitigate or avoid these challenges.
</p><p>There are many conflicting viewpoints on whether all of these are effective or indeed fit the definition of agile development, and this remains an active and ongoing area of research.<sup id="cite_ref-ambler2006_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ambler2006-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oopsla2002_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oopsla2002-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>When agile software development is applied in a distributed setting (with teams dispersed across multiple business locations), it is commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Distributed_agile_software_development" title="Distributed agile software development">distributed agile software development</a>. The goal is to leverage the unique benefits offered by each approach. Distributed development allows organizations to build software by strategically setting up teams in different parts of the globe, virtually building software round-the-clock (more commonly referred to as follow-the-sun model). On the other hand, agile development provides increased transparency, continuous feedback, and more flexibility when responding to changes.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Regulated_domains">Regulated domains</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Regulated domains"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Agile software development methods were initially seen as best suitable for non-critical product developments, thereby excluded from use in regulated domains such as <a href="/wiki/Medical_device" title="Medical device">medical devices</a>, pharmaceutical, financial, nuclear systems, automotive, and avionics sectors, etc. However, in the last several years, there have been several initiatives for the adaptation of agile methods for these domains.<sup id="cite_ref-Cawley2010_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cawley2010-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McHugh2014_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McHugh2014-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SafeScrum_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SafeScrum-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are numerous standards that may apply in regulated domains, including <a href="/wiki/ISO_26262" title="ISO 26262">ISO 26262</a>, <a href="/wiki/ISO_9000" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO 9000">ISO 9000</a>, <a href="/wiki/ISO_9001" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO 9001">ISO 9001</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_15504" title="ISO/IEC 15504">ISO/IEC 15504</a>.
A number of key concerns are of particular importance in regulated domains:<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald2013_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald2013-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Quality_assurance" title="Quality assurance">Quality assurance</a> (QA): Systematic and inherent quality management underpinning a controlled professional process and reliability and correctness of product.</li>
<li>Safety and security: Formal planning and risk management to mitigate safety risks for users and securely protecting users from unintentional and malicious misuse.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Traceability" title="Traceability">Traceability</a>: Documentation providing auditable evidence of regulatory compliance and facilitating traceability and investigation of problems.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verification_and_validation" title="Verification and validation">Verification and validation</a> (V&amp;V): Embedded throughout the software development process (e.g. user requirements specification, functional specification, design specification, code review, unit tests, integration tests, system tests).</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Experience_and_adoption">Experience and adoption</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Experience and adoption"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Although agile software development methods can be used with any programming paradigm or language in practice, they were originally closely associated with object-oriented environments such as Smalltalk, Lisp and later Java, C#. The initial adopters of agile methods were usually small to medium-sized teams working on unprecedented systems with requirements that were difficult to finalize and likely to change as the system was being developed. This section describes common problems that organizations encounter when they try to adopt agile software development methods as well as various techniques to measure the quality and performance of agile teams.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Measuring_agility">Measuring agility</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Measuring agility"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Internal_assessments">Internal assessments</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Internal assessments"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <i>Agility measurement index</i>, amongst others, rates developments against five dimensions of product development (duration, risk, novelty, effort, and interaction).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other techniques are based on measurable goals<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and one study suggests that <a href="/wiki/Velocity_(software_development)" title="Velocity (software development)">velocity</a> can be used as a metric of agility. There are also agile self-assessments to determine whether a team is using agile software development practices (Nokia test,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Karlskrona test,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 42 points test).<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Public_surveys">Public surveys</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Public surveys"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One of the early studies reporting gains in quality, productivity, and business satisfaction by using agile software developments methods was a survey conducted by Shine Technologies from November 2002 to January 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A similar survey, the <i>State of Agile</i>, is conducted every year starting in 2006 with thousands of participants from around the software development community. This tracks trends on the perceived benefits of agility, lessons learned, and good practices. Each survey has reported increasing numbers saying that agile software development helps them deliver software faster; improves their ability to manage changing customer priorities; and increases their productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Surveys have also consistently shown better results with agile product development methods compared to classical project management.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In balance, there are reports that some feel that agile development methods are still too young to enable extensive academic research of their success.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Common_agile_software_development_pitfalls">Common agile software development pitfalls</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Common agile software development pitfalls"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Organizations and teams implementing agile software development often face difficulties transitioning from more traditional methods such as <a href="/wiki/Waterfall_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfall development">waterfall development</a>, such as teams having an agile process forced on them.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Art_of_Agile_Development_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Art_of_Agile_Development-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These are often termed <i>agile anti-patterns</i> or more commonly <i>agile smells</i>. Below are some common examples:
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Lack_of_overall_product_design">Lack of overall product design</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Lack of overall product design"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A goal of agile software development is to focus more on producing working software and less on documentation. This is in contrast to waterfall models where the process is often highly controlled and minor changes to the system require significant revision of supporting documentation. However, this does not justify completely doing without any analysis or design at all. Failure to pay attention to design can cause a team to proceed rapidly at first, but then to require significant rework as they attempt to scale up the system. One of the key features of agile software development is that it is iterative. When done correctly, agile software development allows the design to emerge as the system is developed and helps the team discover commonalities  and opportunities for re-use.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Adding_stories_to_an_iteration_in_progress">Adding stories to an iteration in progress</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Adding stories to an iteration in progress"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In agile software development, <i>stories</i> (similar to <a href="/wiki/Use_case" title="Use case">use case</a> descriptions) are typically used to define requirements and an <i>iteration</i> is a short period of time during which the team commits to specific goals.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Adding stories to an iteration in progress is detrimental to a good flow of work. These should be added to the product backlog and prioritized for a subsequent iteration or in rare cases the iteration could be cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-axisagile.com.au_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axisagile.com.au-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This does not mean that a story cannot expand. Teams must deal with new information, which may produce additional tasks for a story. If the new information prevents the story from being completed during the iteration, then it should be carried over to a subsequent iteration. However, it should be prioritized against all remaining stories, as the new information may have changed the story's original priority.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Lack_of_sponsor_support">Lack of sponsor support</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Lack of sponsor support"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Agile software development is often implemented as a grassroots effort in organizations by software development teams trying to optimize their development processes and ensure consistency in the software development life cycle. By not having sponsor support, teams may face difficulties and resistance from business partners, other development teams and management. Additionally, they may suffer without appropriate funding and resources.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This increases the likelihood of failure.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Insufficient_training">Insufficient training</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Insufficient training"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A survey performed by VersionOne found respondents cited insufficient training as the most significant cause for failed agile implementations<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Teams have fallen into the trap of assuming the reduced processes of agile software development compared to other approaches such as waterfall means that there are no actual rules for agile software development. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Product_owner_role_is_not_properly_filled">Product owner role is not properly filled</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Product owner role is not properly filled"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Product_owner" class="mw-redirect" title="Product owner">product owner</a> is responsible for representing the business in the development activity and is often the most demanding role.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A common mistake is to fill the product owner role with someone from the development team. This requires the team to make its own decisions on prioritization without real feedback from the business. They try to solve business issues internally or delay work as they reach outside the team for direction. This often leads to distraction and a breakdown in collaboration.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Teams_are_not_focused">Teams are not focused</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Teams are not focused"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Agile software development requires teams to meet product commitments, which means they should focus on work for only that product. However, team members who appear to have spare capacity are often expected to take on other work, which makes it difficult for them to help complete the work to which their team had committed.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Excessive_preparation/planning"><span id="Excessive_preparation.2Fplanning"></span>Excessive preparation/planning</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Excessive preparation/planning"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Teams may fall into the trap of spending too much time preparing or planning. This is a common trap for teams less familiar with agile software development where the teams feel obliged to have a complete understanding and specification of all stories. Teams should be prepared to move forward with only those stories in which they have confidence, then during the iteration continue to discover and prepare work for subsequent iterations (often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Refinement_(computing)" title="Refinement (computing)">backlog refinement</a> or grooming).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Problem-solving_in_the_daily_standup">Problem-solving in the daily standup</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Problem-solving in the daily standup"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A daily standup should be a focused, timely meeting where all team members disseminate information. If problem-solving occurs, it often can involve only certain team members and potentially is not the best use of the entire team's time. If during the daily standup the team starts diving into problem-solving, it should be set aside until a sub-team can discuss, usually immediately after the standup completes.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Assigning_tasks">Assigning tasks</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Assigning tasks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One of the intended benefits of agile software development is to empower the team to make choices, as they are closest to the problem. Additionally, they should make choices as close to implementation as possible, to use more timely information in the decision. If team members are assigned tasks by others or too early in the process, the benefits of localized and timely decision making can be lost.<sup id="cite_ref-Effective_Sprint_Planning_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effective_Sprint_Planning-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Being assigned work also constrains team members into certain roles (for example, team member A must always do the database work), which limits opportunities for cross-training.<sup id="cite_ref-Effective_Sprint_Planning_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effective_Sprint_Planning-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Team members themselves can choose to take on tasks that stretch their abilities and provide cross-training opportunities.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Scrum_master_as_a_contributor">Scrum master as a contributor</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Scrum master as a contributor"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the Scrum framework, which claims to be consistent with agile values and principles, the <i>scrum master</i> role is accountable for ensuring the scrum process is followed and for coaching the scrum team through that process. A common pitfall is for a scrum master to act as a contributor. While not prohibited by the Scrum framework, the scrum master needs to ensure they have the capacity to act in the role of scrum master first and not work on development tasks. A scrum master's role is to facilitate the process rather than create the product.<sup id="cite_ref-agileconnection.com_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agileconnection.com-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Having the scrum master also multitasking may result in too many context switches to be productive. Additionally, as a scrum master is responsible for ensuring roadblocks are removed so that the team can make forward progress, the benefit gained by individual tasks moving forward may not outweigh roadblocks that are deferred due to lack of capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Lack_of_test_automation">Lack of test automation</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Lack of test automation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Test_automation" title="Test automation">Test automation</a></div>
<p>Due to the iterative nature of agile development, multiple rounds of testing are often needed. Automated testing helps reduce the impact of repeated unit, integration, and regression tests and frees developers and testers to focus on higher value work.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Test automation also supports continued <a href="/wiki/Refactoring" class="mw-redirect" title="Refactoring">refactoring</a> required by iterative software development. Allowing a developer to quickly run tests to confirm refactoring has not modified the functionality of the application may reduce the workload and increase confidence that cleanup efforts have not introduced new defects.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Allowing_technical_debt_to_build_up">Allowing technical debt to build up</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Allowing technical debt to build up"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Technical_debt" title="Technical debt">Technical debt</a></div>
<p>Focusing on delivering new functionality may result in increased <a href="/wiki/Technical_debt" title="Technical debt">technical debt</a>. The team must allow themselves time for defect remediation and refactoring. Technical debt hinders planning abilities by increasing the amount of unscheduled work as production defects distract the team from further progress.<sup id="cite_ref-Technical_Debt_+_Red_October_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Technical_Debt_+_Red_October-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As the system evolves it is important to <a href="/wiki/Code_refactoring" title="Code refactoring">refactor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Over time the lack of constant maintenance causes increasing defects and development costs.<sup id="cite_ref-Technical_Debt_+_Red_October_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Technical_Debt_+_Red_October-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Attempting_to_take_on_too_much_in_an_iteration">Attempting to take on too much in an iteration</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Attempting to take on too much in an iteration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A common misconception is that agile software development allows continuous change, however an iteration backlog is an agreement of what work can be completed during an iteration.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Having too much <a href="/wiki/Work_in_process" title="Work in process">work-in-progress (WIP)</a> results in inefficiencies such as <a href="/wiki/Human_multitasking" title="Human multitasking">context-switching</a> and queueing.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The team must avoid feeling pressured into taking on additional work.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Fixed_time,_resources,_scope,_and_quality"><span id="Fixed_time.2C_resources.2C_scope.2C_and_quality"></span>Fixed time, resources, scope, and quality</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Fixed time, resources, scope, and quality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Agile software development fixes time (iteration duration), quality, and ideally resources in advance (though maintaining fixed resources may be difficult if developers are often pulled away from tasks to handle production incidents), while the <a href="/wiki/Scope_(project_management)" title="Scope (project management)">scope</a> remains variable. The customer or product owner often pushes for a fixed scope for an iteration. However, teams should be reluctant to commit to the locked time, resources and scope (commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Project_management_triangle" title="Project management triangle">project management triangle</a>). Efforts to add scope to the fixed time and resources of agile software development may result in decreased quality.<sup id="cite_ref-adeptechllc.com_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adeptechllc.com-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Developer_burnout">Developer burnout</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Developer burnout"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Due to the focused pace and continuous nature of agile practices, there is a heightened risk of burnout among members of the delivery team.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Agile_management">Agile management</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Agile management"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agile_management" title="Agile management">Agile management</a></div>
<p>Agile project management is an iterative development process, where feedback is continuously gathered from users and stakeholders to create the right user experience.  Different methods can be used to perform an agile process, these include <a href="/wiki/Scrum_(software_development)" title="Scrum (software development)">scrum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extreme_programming" title="Extreme programming">extreme programming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lean_software_development" title="Lean software development">lean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kanban_(development)" title="Kanban (development)">kanban</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-europeanbusinessreview.com_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europeanbusinessreview.com-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
The term <i>agile management</i> is applied to an iterative, incremental method of managing the design and build activities of engineering, information technology and other business areas that aim to provide new product or service development in a highly flexible and interactive manner, based on the principles expressed in the <i>Manifesto for Agile Software Development</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Agile project management metrics help reduce confusion, identify weak points, and measure team's performance throughout the development cycle. Supply chain agility is the ability of a supply chain to cope with uncertainty and variability on offer and demand. An agile supply chain can increase and reduce its capacity rapidly, so it can adapt to a fast-changing customer demand. Finally, strategic agility is the ability of an organisation to change its course of action as its environment is evolving. The key for strategic agility is to recognize external changes early enough and to allocate resources to adapt to these changing environments.<sup id="cite_ref-europeanbusinessreview.com_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europeanbusinessreview.com-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Agile X techniques may also be called <a href="/wiki/Extreme_project_management" title="Extreme project management">extreme project management</a>. It is a variant of <a href="/wiki/Iterative_and_incremental_development" title="Iterative and incremental development">iterative life cycle</a><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where <a href="/wiki/Deliverable" title="Deliverable">deliverables</a> are submitted in stages. The main difference between agile and iterative development is that agile methods complete small portions of the deliverables in each delivery cycle (iteration),<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while iterative methods evolve the entire set of deliverables over time, completing them near the end of the project. Both iterative and agile methods were developed as a reaction to various obstacles that developed in more sequential forms of project organization. For example, as technology projects grow in complexity, end users tend to have difficulty defining the long-term requirements without being able to view progressive prototypes. Projects that develop in iterations can constantly gather feedback to help refine those requirements.
</p><p>Agile management also offers a simple framework promoting communication and reflection on past work amongst <a href="/wiki/Project_team" title="Project team">team</a> members.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Teams who were using traditional waterfall planning and adopted the agile way of development typically go through a transformation phase and often take help from agile coaches who help guide the teams through a smoother transformation. There are typically two styles of agile coaching: push-based and pull-based agile coaching. Here a "push-system" can refer to an upfront estimation of what tasks can be fitted into a sprint (pushing work) e.g. typical with scrum; whereas a "pull system" can refer to an environment where tasks are only performed when capacity is available.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Agile management approaches have also been employed and adapted to the business and government sectors. For example, within the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal government of the United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">United States Agency for International Development</a> (USAID) is employing a collaborative project management approach that focuses on incorporating <a href="/wiki/Collaborating,_learning_and_adapting" class="mw-redirect" title="Collaborating, learning and adapting">collaborating, learning and adapting</a> (CLA) strategies to iterate and adapt programming.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Agile methods are mentioned in the <i>Guide to the Project Management Body of Knowledge</i> (<i>PMBOK Guide 6th Edition</i>) under the <b>Product Development Lifecycle</b> definition:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Within a project life cycle, there are generally one or more phases
that are associated with the development of the product, service, or result. These are called a development life cycle (...) Adaptive life cycles are agile, iterative, or incremental. The detailed scope is defined and approved before the start of an iteration. Adaptive life cycles are also referred to as agile or change-driven life cycles.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Applications_outside_software_development">Applications outside software development</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Applications outside software development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Agile_Brazil_2014_conference_entrance.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Agile_Brazil_2014_conference_entrance.jpg/250px-Agile_Brazil_2014_conference_entrance.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Agile_Brazil_2014_conference_entrance.jpg/330px-Agile_Brazil_2014_conference_entrance.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Agile_Brazil_2014_conference_entrance.jpg/500px-Agile_Brazil_2014_conference_entrance.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption>Agile Brazil 2014 conference</figcaption></figure>
<p>According to Jean-Loup Richet (research fellow at <a href="/wiki/ESSEC" class="mw-redirect" title="ESSEC">ESSEC</a> Institute for Strategic Innovation &amp; Services) "this approach can be leveraged effectively for non-software products and for project management in general, especially in areas of innovation and uncertainty." The result is a product or project that best meets current customer needs and is delivered with minimal costs, waste, and time, enabling companies to achieve bottom line gains earlier than via traditional approaches.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Agile software development methods have been extensively used for development of software products and some of them use certain characteristics of software, such as <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_analysis_and_design" title="Object-oriented analysis and design">object technologies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith2007_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2007-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, these techniques can be applied to the development of non-software products, such as computers, medical devices, food, clothing, and music.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Agile software development methods have been used in non-development <a href="/wiki/IT_infrastructure" title="IT infrastructure">IT infrastructure</a> <a href="/wiki/IT_infrastructure_deployment" title="IT infrastructure deployment">deployments and migrations</a>. Some of the wider principles of agile software development have also found application in general management<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (e.g., strategy, governance, risk, finance) under the terms <a href="/wiki/Business_agility" title="Business agility">business agility</a> or agile business management. Agile software methodologies have also been adopted for use with the <a href="/wiki/Learning_engineering" title="Learning engineering">learning engineering</a> process, an iterative data-informed process that applies the <a href="/wiki/Learning_sciences" title="Learning sciences">learning sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human-centered_design" title="Human-centered design">human-centered design</a>, and data informed decision-making to support learners and their development.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Agile software development paradigms can be used in other areas of life such as raising children. Its success in child development might be founded on some basic management principles; communication, adaptation, and awareness. In a <a href="/wiki/TED_(conference)" title="TED (conference)">TED Talk</a>, Bruce Feiler shared how he applied basic agile paradigms to household management and raising children.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Criticism">Criticism</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Agile_software_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Criticism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Agile practices have been cited as potentially inefficient in large organizations and certain types of development.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many organizations believe that agile software development methodologies are too extreme and adopt a hybrid approach<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that mixes elements of agile software development and plan-driven approaches.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some methods, such as <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_systems_development_method" title="Dynamic systems development method">dynamic systems development method</a> (DSDM) attempt this in a disciplined way, without sacrificing fundamental principles.
</p><p>The increasing adoption of agile practices has also been criticized as being a <a href="/wiki/Management_fad" title="Management fad">management fad</a> that simply describes existing good practices under new jargon, promotes a <i>one size fits all</i> mindset towards development strategies, and wrongly emphasizes method over results.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alistair_Cockburn" title="Alistair Cockburn">Alistair Cockburn</a> organized a celebration of the 10th anniversary of the <i>Manifesto for Agile Software Development</i> in Snowbird, Utah on 12 February 2011, gathering some 30+ people who had been involved at the original meeting and since. A list of about 20 <a href="/wiki/Elephant_in_the_room" title="Elephant in the room">elephants in the room</a> ('undiscussable' agile topics/issues) were collected, including aspects: the alliances, failures and limitations of agile software development practices and context (possible causes: commercial interests, decontextualization, no obvious way to make progress based on failure, limited objective evidence, cognitive biases and reasoning fallacies), politics and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-TeenAgile_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TeenAgile-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Kruchten" title="Philippe Kruchten">Philippe Kruchten</a> wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712" /><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The agile movement is in some ways a bit like a teenager: very self-conscious, checking constantly its appearance in a mirror, accepting few criticisms, only interested in being with its peers, rejecting en bloc all wisdom from the past, just because it is from the past, adopting fads and new jargon, at times cocky and arrogant. But I have no doubts that it will mature further, become more open to the outside world, more reflective, and therefore, more effective.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8202;<cite>Philippe Kruchten<sup id="cite_ref-TeenAgile_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TeenAgile-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The "Manifesto" may have had a negative impact on higher education management and leadership, where it suggested to administrators that slower traditional and deliberative processes should be replaced with more "nimble" ones. The concept rarely found acceptance among university faculty.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another criticism is that in many ways, agile management and traditional management practices end up being in opposition to one another. A common criticism of this practice is that the time spent attempting to learn and implement the practice is too costly, despite potential benefits. A transition from traditional management to agile management requires total submission to agile and a firm commitment from all members of the organization to seeing the process through. Issues like unequal results across the organization, too much change for employees' ability to handle, or a lack of guarantees at the end of the transformation are just a few examples.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFLeeXia2010" class="citation journal cs1">Lee, Gwanhoo; Xia, Weidong (2010). </li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFKrollRichardsonPrikladnickiAudy2018" class="citation journal cs1">Kroll, J.; Richardson, I.; Prikladnicki, R.; Audy, J. L. (2018). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016/j.infsof.2017.08.011">"Empirical evidence in follow the Sun software development: A systematic mapping study"</a>. <i>Information and Software Technology</i>. <b>93</b>: <span class="nowrap">30–</span>44. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.infsof.2017.08.011">10.1016/j.infsof.2017.08.011</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hdl_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hdl (identifier)">hdl</a>:<span class="id-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://hdl.handle.net/10344%2F6233">10344/6233</a></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Information+and+Software+Technology&amp;rft.atitle=Empirical+evidence+in+follow+the+Sun+software+development%3A+A+systematic+mapping+study&amp;rft.volume=93&amp;rft.pages=%3Cspan+class%3D%22nowrap%22%3E30-%3C%2Fspan%3E44&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft_id=info%3Ahdl%2F10344%2F6233&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.infsof.2017.08.011&amp;rft.aulast=Kroll&amp;rft.aufirst=J.&amp;rft.au=Richardson%2C+I.&amp;rft.au=Prikladnicki%2C+R.&amp;rft.au=Audy%2C+J.+L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1016%2Fj.infsof.2017.08.011&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAgile+software+development" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-craig2003-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-craig2003_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Gerald_M._Weinberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerald M. Weinberg">Gerald M. Weinberg</a>, as quoted in <a href="#CITEREFLarmanBasili2003">Larman &amp; Basili 2003</a>, pp.&#160;47–56 "We were doing incremental development as early as 1957 in Los Angeles, under the direction of Bernie Dimsdale at <a href="/wiki/Service_Bureau_Corporation" title="Service Bureau Corporation">IBM's Service Bureau Corporation</a>. He was a colleague of <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a>, so perhaps he learned it there, or assumed it as totally natural. I do remember Herb Jacobs (primarily, though we all participated) developing a large simulation for Motorola, where the technique used was, as far as I can tell&#160;... All of us, as far as I can remember, thought waterfalling of a huge project was rather stupid, or at least ignorant of the realities. I think what the waterfall description did for us was make us realize that we were doing something else, something unnamed except for 'software development.'"</span>
</li>
